Which wouldn't be so bad if shadows, for the most part, weren't black.  Some production design detail and face detail,especially inside the vampire strip bar, are blacked out and indecipherable.  So, if you don't care about special features you will be in a very mild version of heaven. The Alliance Canadian release, somehow, looks better and cloer to what the original film looked like, which is really weird considering their print it most likely where Echo Bridge got their master. B003Y74AXO|4.0|Even though the iPad's screen is very hard glass, after nine months of use I started to accumulate small scratches. Time for a screen protector. Amazon Vine offered me a free one and I jumped.     Screen protectors are a challenge to install without bubbles. This amazon basic product is no exception. I've put screen protectors on my iPhones twice before, but this was my first attempt on my iPad. As always, clean the screen with the included microfiber cloth - better yet buy a pack of Zwipes microfiber cloths from amazon (I use these on my iPad, in the car, in the kitchen, etc). After that, importantly, work in a low dust environment (no fan, open windows, crowd of people, etc). Then follow the directions.     My first shot yielded five small bubbles. These were actually specks of dust that had settled on the screen and/or film's adhesive when I was positioning the protector. No need to throw the protector away and start again. Take a mechanical pencil - or other similar implement with a sharp metal tip - and flick the dust off. Several of the dust specks would not separate from the film's adhesive and I had to manually move the dust specks with the tip of my mechanical pencil several inches to the edge before being able to removing them. This took several minutes, during which another two dust specks settled. Repeated the process. Made mental note to recommend working in a dust free/dust reduced environment.     What's cool is that with the protector now on - sans bubbles - I can't see the fine scratches that are on the iPad's glass anymore! So don't have to worry about old scratches or new scratches. Awesome.

The standouts from this album to me were tracks 1, 6, and 10 to 13, where Yanni experimented with new rhythms and accompaniment styles and produced songs that are much more complex and impressionable then any of his works in the 2000's. I even found myself looping several of his songs like what I did back in the days of LATA.    However, while they are nice to listen to, these songs still lack the powerfully unique melodic themes pre-LATA albums and LATA had (for example, Santorini, Acroyali/Standing in Motion, Keys to Imagination), which made them the unforgettable classics that they are. I know this is a studio album but even compared to pre-LATA studio albums, I still found that unique Yanni musical signature lacking here. These songs are just songs, the style of which may or may not translate well to full orchestration, while pre-LATA songs easily exuded an epic sensation that was amplified exponentially when they were performed with a full orchestra. This is a source of some dissapointment.     There is a reason why LATA is one of the best selling music videos of all time but Yanni for some reason seems to be deliberately straying away from it. I would be very interested in hearing the symphonic versions of these songs, hoping that would recreate at least some of the brilliance of LATA, and rediscover that unique \\"Yanniness\\" that made me a Yanni fan in the first place.

After which Novinha, grief-stricken at the loss of this man who has become her second father, calls off-world for the services of a Speaker for the Dead. She retracts her request shortly afterward, but not before it reaches Andrew Wiggin on Trondheim.<br /><br />No one on Trondheim, or elsewhere for that matter, knows that Andrew Wiggin is actually 3000 years old - not 35, as he appears thanks to the effects of star travel. No one but his sister, Valentine, who has married on Trondheim after following her beloved little brother everywhere since \\"Ender\\" Wiggin destroyed the alien buggers' home-world and earned himself first Humankind's gratitude...and then, the shameful title of Ender the Xenocide. For the buggers actually meant Humans no harm, and until now no other fellow sentient species has been discovered.<br /><br />Andrew will not ask pregnant Valentine to come with him one more time. The 22-year journey to Lusitania passes, for him, in only a few days. Which means that he finds not the young girl Novinha, but a woman newly widowed and embittered by secrets that threaten to destroy her six children as well as Novinha herself. Uncovering secrets is one of the greatest skills the original Speaker for the Dead possesses. Not only Novinha's secrets, but those of Lusitania and its natives, too.<br /><br />This is the best Orson Scott Card book I have yet read. Its world-building works beautifully, and its characters manage just the right balance between being flawed and sympathetic. The best, part, though, is the alienness of the piggies. Just when they seem so easy to understand in Human terms - that's when they turn out to be anything else but. A fantastic read if you enjoy imaginative SF!<br /><br />--Reviewed by Nina M. Osier, author of 2005 science fiction EPPIE winner \\"Regs\\"t read. Its world-building works beautifully, and its characters manage just the right balance between being flawed and sympathetic. B002ZG9800|5.0|I LOVE YOU PHILLIP MORRIS is a rare film, one that hopefully will draw enough audience support because of the participation of  Jim Carrey and Ewan McGregor, two of cinema's favorite actors taking on a project that, while controversial to some, ends up being one of the best and most tender love stories on film. The bizarre aspect of this film is that it is based on a book by Steven McVicker whose life story is revealed in the autobiographical book 'I Love You Phillip Morris: A True Story of Life, Love, and Prison Breaks'. The author's true name is Steven Jay Russell, born 1957, a US con artist and impostor, known for escaping from prison multiple times, earning numerous nicknames, including \\"Houdini\\" and \\"King Con\\".  The book was adapted for the screen and directed by Glenn Ficarra and John Requa who have been successful in transferring the flavor and the facts of the book into a movie that is part comedy, part drama, and part social commentary.<br /><br />After a brief but meaningful flashback to his youth the film opens with Steven Russell (Jim Carrey, in a career defining role) as a policeman married to hyper-religious Debbie (Leslie Mann) and father to a loving daughter. Steven knows he was adopted and in his role as a policeman he has access to records that bring to finding his biological mother (Barbara Bascombe) who denies his existence.  Steven is crushed and realizing he has been living a life of a constant lie (he has known since childhood that he is gay) has an automobile wreck, survives, but decides to inform his wife that he is gay.  The family moves from Georgia to Texas where Steven wiles his way through jobs as a con artist, finally living an 'out' life with boyfriend Jimmy (the brilliant Brazilian actor Rodrigo Santoro who is far too underused in American films).  Finding that he cannot support his expensive tastes on his salary he begins his Con Artist career, eventually ending in prison where he adjusts to life, works the system, and falls in love with a sweet little 'blue eyed blond' Phillip Morris (Ewan McGregor).  The attraction between the two men is credible and the manner in which Carrey and McGregor create these roles is sincere, healthy, and for once not a parody of gay relationships but instead is a case study in healthy love relationships.  The remainder of the film is a series of tricks and manipulations by Steven to get released from prison so that he can get Phillip released.  It is a series of events that were it not fact would leave the audience saying 'that's not possible', but the story is true and the manner in which the story ends is a revelation.<br /><br />The film is a fine study of the mind of a brilliant con artist coupled with a very tender tale of love and devotion between the two main characters.  The script is excellent with many moments of dialogue that should be quoted but would alter the joy of hearing them fresh as the viewer becomes involved with the characters. Jim Carrey gives his best performance ever, Ewan McGregor continues to impress with the spectrum of his acting skills, and the supporting cast, even in the small cameos is exemplary.  This is a very fine film that deserves a wide audience - for many, many reasons.  Grady Harp, April 11with a sweet little 'blue eyed blond' Phillip Morris (Ewan McGregor). This could have been written in movements, which would have made it easier to, maybe, get a few playable edits for on air performance. But this wasn't the goal of the group, or of prog groups of the time. Other reviewers had mentioned Genesis \\"The Lamb Lies Down On Broadway\\" from 1974 but, this was actually released in the UK in '73 so it was written and recorded around their \\"Selling England by the Pound\\", GG's \\"Free Hand\\", Tull's \\"A Passion Play\\" and ELP's \\"Brain Salad Surgery\\", to name a few of the players in the prog arena. Tull's offering being the one closest to this format, albeit a single album, but only 1 piece, split in two by comic relief \\"The Hare Who Lost His Spectacles\\". Some would argue that Tull is not to be included in this category, but look at their breadth of work. Who knows what these musicians would have done with today's technology, and recording limits. This is indeed the same for this Yes work. Limited only by format, and the time provided on a side of vinyl, to showcase their compositional prowess.   Musically diverse, well composed and performed, but showing the physical limits of, especially, Steve Howe's playing. Dexterity on the quicker passages are not his forte', but if you find a smoother player for these, you'd lose the composition and style of Steve, which is a signature of Yes. His slower, ethereal licks, and acoustic parts are to be vigorously applauded. Rick Wakeman is the backseat leader in this work. I say \\"backseat\\" as Steve's playing dominates the mix up front. Rick is probably the most accomplished player, next to Chris Squire, who's performances are both on point. But take a supporting role, with little time to stretch, even in this lengthy compositional offering. They drive and lead the composition's directions around the difficult lyrical cadences andsometimes self-indulgent guitar. Keyboards are crucial to Yes' compositions, as Tony Banks' are to Genesis. Chris' performance is fantastic as usual. As the other half of the rhythm section, Alan White's performance is no standout. But, just to play this type of music in the first place, makes you no slouch. You've heard this music before, and after, just never this much at one sitting. \\"BURP\\" I'm full.  Now, I'm more of a musician's musician, I prefer instrumentals, and vocals, can get in the way of a proper composition. (my opinion) They have their place, and Jon Anderson delivers them in his breathy, somewhat weak, style. His vocal style IS Yes, for better or worse. Prose are tough to put to music. Lyrics here are something I only follow for the melody, difficult to understand in the mix at times, and set too far apart for congruity. If his vocals were more upfront in the mix it would detract from the finished product. His vocal limitations too apparent. How much \\" La la la la la\\" can you listen to? This is the case in \\"Ritual...\\" I guess he needed something to do during 20 minutes. Melody and harmonies are Yes. And I like the sound, but I don't care what they're saying. Certainly not going to sing along to this type of music.   Overall a strong group statement, in a format that will probably never be realized again. If it can't be sung along to, or danced to within a 3 minute time period, no $$$ for you! Can't sell it! The illiterate and mediocre have money to spend. If you are one of the aforementioned folks, run. Run away screaming, as this would make your little head explode. Leave this, as well as anything important, to those of us who can comprehend and appreciate. There . . . I said it!  So put this in your collection. Try to put 2 hours on hold and listen to it without interruption. Tough, I know. But worth a shot. Like I said before... my opinion.

B004BLJQOK|5.0|Let Me In is the American independent film remake of the highly regarded Swedish film of 2008, [[ASIN:B001MYIXAC Let The Right One In]].  After having watched both films, they tackle the same basic plot and subject matter from slightly different angles, but are both worthy of admiration.<br /><br />In my mind the overall feeling of Let the Right One In is one of isolation of the main characters, Oskar and Eli.  The stark winter landscapes, washed out colors, and lack of emotion shown my almost all of the protagonists add to this feeling of isolation, dreariness, and despair.  While Let Me In certainly spends a portion of it's time on the isolation of Owen, who has no connection to anyone else until Abby moves into his life, it also shows the terror that Owen lives in.  I think that Let Me In does a better job of detailing the fear and desperation that Owen feels -  fear and desperation that allows him to justify his increasing violent actions (or acceptance of such actions) in the film.  Until the penultimate scene of Let the Right One In, I never really felt that Oskar was that oppressed by the school bullies, and I didn't understand where all of the hatred and thirst for revenge against them was coming from.<br /><br />The other thing I noticed when comparing the two films was that Let Me In did a finer jobs of detailing the budding relationship between Owen and Abby better than Let the Right One In did with Oskar and Eli.  I was never fully convinced at the end of Let the Right One In why Oskar and Eli ended up together, while I understood perfectly why Owen chose to stay with Abby even though he understood the implications for his future.  Perhaps this was due to the outstanding performances by Kodi Smit-McPhee and Chlo Grace Moretz in the lead roles; perhaps this was just due to a bit being lost in translation from the Swedish language film.  I just felt the connection between Owen and Abby a bit more than that between Oskar and Eli.<br /><br />Now this is not meant to denigrate Let the Right One In; I think that the original can be a bit lethargic at times but is nevertheless a fine movie and deserving of its accolades. Basically the movie tells the story about a boy, Mowgli, who is raised by wolves in the jungles of Madhya Pradesh, India. It is ten years later and the tribe of wolves feels that the man cub must be sent to the human village because Shere Khan, a tiger, has return and they feel that they cannot protect the boy. The movie version of the Jungle Book differs greatly from the book version. In the book version there is no King Louie no vultures and the only resemblance to the book is the names used in the movie. Walt thought the book was too dark for children and instructed his animation department not to read the book. The Jungle Book was nominate for an Academy Award in 1967 for Best Song, The Bare Necessities, but lost out to Doctor Dolittle for Talk to the Animals. Gregory Peck tried to get the movie nominated and win for the Best Picture category, but the members of the Academy of Motion Pictures and Science felt that animation films did not belong in that category. B004HO6I4M|3.0|\\"TEN out of ten for style, but minus several million for good thinking,\\" as Zaphod Beeblebrox might say, to the marketers of Rio, or whoever it was who conceived the strapline \\"this turkey can't fly\\".<br /><br />Well, you're asking for it, aren't you. And for no reason: Blu, the flightless bird in question, isn't even a turkey. So I attended the screening in Leicester Square, armed with discerning co-critics in the shape of my children, and figured that, worst case, at least I had a title for my review.<br /><br />The kids enjoyed it happily enough. I didn't. It wasn't a turkey, as such - Turkeys are fun to watch the same way Candid Camera is - but more of a damp, er, squab. (Sorry folks). For any parent who has owned a DVD player in the last decade, Rio will feel very, very, tiringly familiar.<br /><br />I like reviewing. I take it seriously, and I try to come up with a novel angle and provide new and thoughtful remarks for each picture I see. This is sometimes hard, but on occasions like this, it makes me positively resentful: why should I expend imaginative thought on reviewing a picture when the film makers themselves didn't bother when making the damn thing? Why don't I just create a review-by-numbers?<br /><br />Now it's no secret that Hollywood has a \\"magic formula\\" for its successful dramas, bequeathed to it fifty or more years ago by Joseph Campbell in a still-referenced book called \\"The Hero With A Thousand Faces\\". Indeed, it has been suggested that there are only seven basic plots in all of literature. Certainly, most popular commercial films (even serious ones) cleave more or less closely to one of these fictional archetypes.<br /><br />But of late this adherence to formula seems to have gone to a whole new place, especially in this new genre, the digitally animated children's movie. It's a genre I declare officially mature, established and subject to rapidly advancing decrepitude. Rio (and Mars Needs Moms) are my evidence for that claim.<br /><br />Nowadays it is not just plot but scene, casting, characterisation, colourisation and disposition that are done by rote: you fancy someone at Disney has drawn up something like a pilot's checklist for script vetting. Each new offering at most adds a new trope or contrivance to the library of available memes. As the library grows, the more memes there are to chose from, of course, and the less incentive there is to create new ones. The assembly of a film in this way becomes modular; a matter of supply-chaining.<br /><br />So it is with Rio. It is a classic journey of self discovery. Blu, a Brazilian macaw hatchling, is snatched from his nest and whisked away to captivity far from his native land (cf. [[ASIN:B00007KGCW Finding Nemo]]). He even has a Nemo-esque physical disability: he can't fly.<br /><br />Of course, he winds up back in Rio, and there contrives to meet a truculent paramour (cf. [[ASIN:B000CEVVIO Stuart Little]]), be captured (again) by dastardly but inept animal traffickers (cf. [[ASIN:B001GN1SWE Garfield]]), escape with the help of fellow captured animals (cf. Nemo again, also cf. [[ASIN:B0042FZOFK Toy Story]]), be captured (again) this time by a dastardly big bird (cf. Stuart Little again), confront his fears, fall in love, while his owner falls in love too, and confronts her fears, and so on and so on.<br /><br />This is accompanied (or interrupted) by a set piece so strikingly resemblant of the jungle boogie in [[ASIN:B0009PAPL0 Madagascar]] that the litigious type might sue over it. Even Avatar is scene-checked. All this in the context of a dull script, badly delivered jokes, and adventure/chase set pieces designed to show off the technical department's digital wizardry (fellas, we get the picture: you can do cool stuff with computers. How about doing some cool stuff with celluloid?) that have the wit and wisdom of your average seven year old.<br /><br />Admittedly, this is aimed at your average seven-year-old but, as Pixar has shown repeatedly, that'sno reason a film can't be brilliantly funny for all ages.<br /><br />In a poor game there is still a man-of-the-match performance from Flight of The Conchords' Jemaine Clement as Nigel, a baddie cockatoo - one of the more convincing screen villains of recent times.<br /><br />I can't deny my children were entertained (by comparison they were positively aggravated by Mars Needs Moms). Therefore it isn't, in all honesty, a turkey. But it doesn't fly.<br /><br />Olly Buxtonre is still a man-of-the-match performance from Flight of The Conchords' Jemaine Clement as Nigel, a baddie cockatoo - one of the more convincing screen villains of recent times. B002GTZSZU|5.0|This film tells the very interesting story of how Antonio Salieri, the court composer in Vienna, tried to compete with Mozart. I stress \\"tried\\" because the film also makes it clear what a genius Mozart was - he started composing operas and symphonies before he was even a teenager, and when composing he did so without needing any corrections. Salieri was jealous of Mozart and appalled when he learned what a depraved man he was. Salieri himself pledged to God he would remain celibate if he was granted the ability to create music.    The story is told through flashbacks by Salieri who is now an old man living in a mental hospital. F. Murray Abraham is wonderful as Salieri - showing his scheming as a younger man and his lasting jealousy and admiration of Mozart as an old man. Even Salieri admired and cherished Mozart's works. The movie contains a great selection of his works, used both as background music and in the foreground with scenes involving his operas performed on stage. Among other things, watching this movie reminded me I want to see another classical concert sometime soon; it even made me interested in going to see an opera.    My favorite part of the movie was Mozart himself. Tom Hulce was dynamic in the role, conveying Mozart's zest for life, an abundance of passion for his work, and his deterioration toward the end. On the spur of the moment, Mozart was able to memorize and improve upon a small piece by Salieri which he heard only once. He fought against any minor changes to his own work because he considered it perfect as it was written. Mozart was rightfully proud of his work, but as a result of his pride and non-conformity, he found it difficult to make a living. Salieri, with the little power he did have, managed to make things even more difficult for Mozart by getting the performances of his operas limited to only a few. Salieri even uses Mozart's feelings of guilt after his father's passing to drive him crazy. While the end is tragic, the movie stillleft me delighted by the wonderful music and how full of life they made Mozart seem.

The premise of the series (a terminally cancerous chemistry teacher selling crystal meth to provide for his family) has long been forgotten by the end of season three, and yet the writers still manage to keep the show evolving at a pace that matches, and even sometimes exceeds, the gripping excitement the show began with.     The acting quality of Breaking Bad's cast is exceptional as well; Once you've seen Bryan Cranston as Walter White, you won't be able to watch him in anything else; his realization of the character completely justifies his three consecutive Emmy wins. The supporting actors are also expertly cast; you will get lost in this show. I understand this all may sound superfluous, but Breaking Bad deserves all the praise it receives. Just stop reading what I think about it and go watch it already! You won't be disappointed.

031261246X|4.0|Geek Thrillers are hard to come by.  The last such thriller I can remember was [[ASIN:1416507787 The Cuckoo's Egg: Tracking a Spy Through the Maze of Computer Espionage]], a novel that focuses on the nitty gritty of tracking down a hacker in the pre-WWW days.    Zero Day is a novel about a security consultant that is hired to fix the damage a virus has caused; and during this time the virus causes havoc with other computer systems as it propagates through the internet, resulting in the deaths of civilians in many walks of life: Hospital patients, nuclear power engineers, Automobile factory workers, Ships running aground, and airplanes in flight.  The virus turns out to be more sinister than first thought; and it's a race against the clock to figure out who made it and how to stop it.    Zero Day is written as if it's going to be turned into a movie. There isn't a lot of backstory or internal thinking; most of the action happens 'on the screen', where it would be relatively simple to turn it into a movie. It's extremely well written, and there were times that I couldn't put it down and if I did I'd just pick it up again.  It delves into the worst-case scenario and shows just how plausible it is.      Mark draws on his real-world experience as a Security researcher at Microsoft to bring the plausible nature of Zero Day to life.  The tools used in Zero Day mirror real-world applications; but unfortunately he doesn't take it to the depths that followers of his blog might expect.  As a programmer I hoped for more technical information about the virus or the tools used to track it down; but the author chose to forgo that to entice a larger audience.      If you're expecting a thriller that goes into the depth that Cuckoo's Egg did, you'll be sorely disappointed. If however, you're expecting an exciting adventure that keeps you hooked until the last page, give Zero Day a read, or five.

0375703861|5.0|I loved reading this book.      Zadie Smith weaves a very funny, poignant and at times heart wrenching story of two families from very different backgrounds who come together as friends.  She expresses the worries many immigrant families feel when they are trying to raise their children in a culture different to their own.  How much do they give up in order fit in, and how much do they hold on to, in order to stay true to their religion, roots and beliefs?  Immigrant parents are often torn, they've usually moved to a new land for a better life, but not everything that comes with the move is to their liking.  The cultures often clash and a struggle between parents, their children, pop culture, schools and community etc. can ensue.  These are the very things that Zadie Smith addresses in \\"White Teeth\\".      A good portion of the story is told through the voice of Samad, a Bengali father who has moved to England and has made friends with an Englishman Archie, and his Jamaican wife Clara.  However, the other part is told through their children and how they feel about the age in which they are living and how they deal with their own angst and growing pains.  Clara's family and other characters add quite a bit of entertainment to the story too.  The way in which Zadie has written the dialogue was fun to read.  As I read, I could hear the accents of the Bengali family, the English characters, the crass youth and the Jamaican patois of Clara and her family.  I found myself laughing out loud many many times throughout the book - once nearly falling off the bike at the gym!    Personally, I'd liked to have known more about Archie and Clara's relationship and how that marriage survived it's differences, but I thoroughly enjoyed living the ups and downs of Samad and his wife Alsana's relationship and the exploits of the children in the book.    Overall, an excellent read!  I feel I may be slightly biased however, as my multicultural background (Indian, Jamaican, English) is main theme of this book - and that NEVER happens, plus it is set in the 70's & 80's in England which is the era of my teenage years, so i could really relate on so many levels!!!   Having said that, this book was recommended to me by a middle-aged white American woman - so i guess it's enjoyable to a broad range of people!

0756404738|5.0|I cannot for the life of me understand how anyone can write a review of this novel and complain that it was too long. Think of \\"Lord of the Rings\\".  Could that story have been told in one book, in a third of the time?  Of course - but then it wouldn't be the same story.  It wouldn't be as nuanced, the characters wouldn't be as fleshed out, the WORLD wouldn't be as developed.  It's the same with \\"The Wise Man's Fear\\".  Discovering a world is not a quick process that should be rushed through to get to the plot, and if Rothfuss' had done that, if he'd edited and cut as some of the negative reviews are saying he should have, this novel wouldn't be half as incredible as it is.

B000006TRV|5.0|Pink Floyd was already on top of the world when they released this album which, in a way, is the most audacious record they ever recorded.  This album has more of a unified concept than anything else in the Pink Floyd discography.  A rock opera much like The Who's \\"Tommy\\", and Genesis' \\"The Lamb Lies Down On Broadway\\", this album tells the story of a troubled rock star who becomes emotionally detached and downright sadistic.  The album works much like a film: the protagonist, named Pink, goes through a tragic story of metal decline, and eventually redemption.  He slowly builds a \\"wall\\" around himself, completely shutting himself from the world outside his mind.    Much of the story is based on Roger Water own life.  Like Pink, he lost a father during the war, and also like Pink, he became a emotionally disturbed rock star.  The main character's story is also much like that of former member Syd Barrett, who left the band after metal illness and drug abuse caught up to him.  The album take on a very depressing tone as the protagonist's life spirals out of control.  Every horrific event in his life such as the death of his father, the abuse of school teachers, and his divorce install another \\"brick\\" in the wall.  The concept is unlike anything ever thought of in rock music.  You don't even see this kind of story much in film with the exception of a few like \\"A Clock Work Orange\\" and \\"Taxi Driver\\"; both of which have plots similar to \\"The Wall\\".    This plot, however, would not work well if the music wasn't anything exceptional.  Yet, the music is some of the best Pink Floyd has ever recorded.  Aside from the usual eerie soundscapes, their is a lot of dialog that moves the story along.  And then there's David Gilmour's guitar work.  His solos on \\"Hey You\\", and especially \\"Comfortably Numb\\" have become classic rock standards.  And of course there's the famous childrens chorus on the second part of \\"Another Brick In The Wall\\", which helped elevate that song to legendary status.    This is about as mature as classic rock can get.  It has a message, and it goes to the extreme to convey that message to its listeners.  The message that redemption can come even for the most destructive of souls is an age old rhetoric that is creatively presented here is an effortless fashion.  A must have for those who like to \\"think\\" when they listen to rock.

Starting from childhood and a modeling job she was sure she talked her way into, and ending with the fairy tale romance we see from her and Ellen today. Portia spent most of her life feeling like she didn't deserve anything that came her way This was partially because she was gay, and because she couldn't change that, she controlled the one thing she could: her weight. She lived two lives, one in front of the cameras where she was a single straight female on a hit TV series. Another, a closeted gay woman obsessed with food portions and working out. She lived a sick sad life of self abuse and loneliness until she was able to confront her family and herself with the reality of her illness and her lifestyle.<br /><br />I am absolutely in love with this book and with Portia. Before reading this book I too have struggled with my weight. It's funny because she says something to the effect of: No matter how much I diet or workout I always end up right back at 130. This ended up being her \\"natural weight\\", something I had heard of a person having but I always struggled with finding or knowing mine. I had tried everything from fad diets to not eating until 5pm, and I always remained at 130. Food was an obsession, and I would obsess over what was in my cabinets until I allowed myself to eat them. I would binge, but unlike Portia, I never purged. Since reading this compelling story I have ended my battle with food. I enjoy what I want when I want. I never considered myself to have an eating disorder, but I definitely spend way too much time thinking about eating. Since reading this book I have ended that cycle of thoughts. I related a lot to Portia when she would tell herself that she didn't deserve things because she was fat or disgusting and there were even times when I was jealous of her control when she only ate a small amount of calories a day. I actually felt motivated by her sense of urgency towards working out and almost wondered if this book was going to be a tool towards me being worse with food then better. However, when I reached the final pages of the book I felt freedom from food because she felt freedom from food. It was such an emotional journey, one that I flew through with her, and by the end it just made sense to stop fighting with food and to just let go. That was three months ago. Since then I have not gained a pound. I do not binge, and I am no longer hating myself after I eat a meal. I recommend this book to anyone who has ever battled with food, and to everyone else who hasn't.motivated by her sense of urgency towards working out and almost wondered if this book was going to be a tool towards me being worse with food then better. Whereas before you would only be using 10% of your brain now you would be using all of your brain. And this miracle drug changes Eddie's life, transforming him into a genius with hitherto unknown levels of motivation: in 4 days he finishes the book he has been trying to write for months, charms the landlord's wife so he doesn't get thrown out of his apartment (and sleeps with her), learns how to fight from recalling martial arts movies he saw on TV, lands a job as a stockbroker, gets back with his girlfriend, starts making new and wealthy friends, starts appreciating (and finding useful) mathematics, discovers he can quickly learn anything he puts his mind to and gains an appetite for culture. But all is dependent on the pill: when the effects of the pill wear off he goes back to being the old Eddie. Eddie has a stash of the NZT-48 pills because he retrieved them from Vernon's apartment after discovering that he had been murdered shortly after they met. But now the same people that killed his brother-in-law are after him because they want the drug. But Eddie is ambitious and wants to make his mark on the world. He cannot stop taking the drug. He wants to invest money but the banks won't lend so he borrows $100,000 from a Russian gangster / loan shark who threatens to `flay him alive' if he doesn't repay. When he meets with his ex-wife Melissa Gant (played by Anna Friel) who informs him that she used to also take NZT-48 and it nearly killed her, Eddie must decide: is risking his life worth becoming limitless? Furthermore an important business deal for business mogul Carl Van Loon (played by Robert De Niro) who he works for is in danger of going belly-up, and Eddie needs this deal to succeed if he is going to get to where he wants in life. And when the Russian gangster that lent him money comes to collect and gets Eddie's last NZT-48 pill off of him and takes it and becomes a genius himself, Eddie's life just might be forfeit because the gangster wants more of the drug and will kill Eddie if he doesn't get it. Will Eddie survive all of these challenges and achieve his goals? A most enjoyable way to pass a couple of hours. Nonetheless the film holds your attention throughout and has a most fascinating premise: what would you do if you were limitless? 0756404738|5.0|Writing a review for The Wise Man's Fear, Book Two of Patrick Rothfuss's Kingkiller Chronicle, is a difficult task for me. First, the book is 994 pages in length and covers so many plot-lines that it's hard to create a manageable review. Second, there was considerable hype leading up to the release of the book, hype that I well bought into and dabbled with. Third is the fact that this is the middle volume of a trilogy and the story, in the long run, is still unread. Finally, and probably most importantly, is my bias towards Rothfuss. If not for his genre-crushing, decade favorite Name of the Wind, I may have given up on fantasy a few years ago. Instead, the man pulled the wool from my eyes and revealed that a story is more important than magic and cliches. That said, this review is spoiler-free for Book 2, though it will contain spoilers from Book 1.<br /><br />-----<br />Review (No WMF Spoilers)<br />It's another day for Kvothe, his student/assistant Bast, and the acclaimed scribe Devan Lochees (aka the Chronicler) at the Waystone Inn. All more than a little burdened from the recent death of Shep and the unusual circumstances surrounding it, Kvothe is pressing on to tell the true story of the man behind the legends. Times are hard for Newarre and its poor farmers, but times are hard for everyone.<br /><br />Rothfuss wastes no time getting into the meat of the story. By Chapter Three, Kvothe is recalling the familiar story that began in Name of the Wind. In fact, if physical evidence didn't say otherwise, the transition between Book One and Book Two is flawless enough that it feels like I'm reading one book, carrying over the same tone and voice the first one did so well.<br /><br />If one considers The Wise Man's Fear on its own, problems arise. In its 994 pages a lot of stuff happens, and on the other hand, nothing happens. The reader gets plenty of insight to the Four Corners of Civilization, from language lessons in Ademre to lessons of the court in Severen, as well as more lore and history of all manner of folks between. There is enough world building that one would be tempted to say Rothfuss is wasting too much time with supplemental information, putting off the more important elements of the story. And, if one considers WMF on its own apart from the trilogy as a whole, I would agree.<br /><br />There is not enough action and the story is not tight enough for this book to fit the normal molds and expectations readers have. But, just as Rothfuss is not interested in telling a normal story with normal cliches, this book rises above the normal expectations. I cannot see how anyone can take a single volume of a story instead of the whole and weigh it and judge it, not fairly, but it happens. For myself, taking everything I learned in NotW and adding it to WMF, I see one beautiful tale, written in prose and verse sweet enough to charm anyone interested in a good story.<br /><br />And that's what Wise Man's Fear is. Better, even, than \\"good.\\" It's complex, elegant, hilarious, devastating, tense, dark, mysterious, and many more adjectives. It's not a book that stands on its own, but its the middle piece to a three-piece puzzle. The story is the most important thing, and Rothfuss is spinning a wonderful yarn.<br /><br />All of this is not to say that I didn't have problems with WMF. Do I feel like there was a lot of extraneous stuff? Maybe, but is that necessarily a bad thing? How often do we fall in love with things (book series, tv shows, video games, etc.) and wish we had more? We long for special features and extended scenes. Why else would there be a Saw VI and two video games to boot? Could plenty of this stuff have been edited out and the story still be the same? Yes. But did I enjoy it all? Absolutely.<br /><br />I'm willing to wade through lots of boring stuff if the story's good, and Rothfuss's extra scenes are not boring. In the end, what I can say about Wise Man's Fear is that its long, and if you get frustrated at astory that likes to simmer and slow-boil instead of splash out on the stove, then you may be irked, but you'll be entertained, as well. If you liked The Name of the Wind you'll like this book. I know I did.on the stove, then you may be irked, but you'll be entertained, as well. If you liked The Name of the Wind you'll like this book. I know I did.

031261246X|5.0|Mark Russinovich has done a great job of taking one of the biggest challenges of 21st Century and turning it into a techno-thriller.  Natural disasters, dirty bombs, suitcase nukes, and bio-agents all have the potential of causing terror, yet are only likely to affect a small fraction of the world's population.  But the reality Russinovich conveys is that the computer systems embedded in most every aspect of our lives are vulnerable to cyber-attack.  The capability to reach out and take control of these systems can be safely exercised from anywhere on the planet with devastating effect at the global scale.  Attackers don't need to pass through airport security.  And the programming work required to create attacks can apparently be outsourced!  Worried about social security going bankrupt?  Maybe we should be more worried that the electronic records representing the world's wealth could be suddenly wiped out along with all backup copies.    The last cyber-based novel I read was Digital Fortress -- and I found the many technical errors extremely distracting.  This wasn't a problem with Zero Day.  Russinovich clearly knows his stuff when it comes to computer technology, and brings a chilling element of realism while at the same time telling a compelling story.    There are a few places where the book is gimicky (maybe engineers shouldn't write sex scenes), and parts of the story followed the standard formula of the genre.  But most of Russinovich's characters are more intriguing and better developed than with many novice novelists.  He avoided making them caricatures, yet uses them to move the plot along at a rapid pace.    In Zero Day, we dodge a bullet.  I hope Russinovich follows up with a sequel that explains how we can all start to feel safe again, no longer living under the ominous cloud of vulnerability to cyber-attack he warns us about in this novel.

0393338827|4.0|Michael Lewis has turned in one of his usual great performances in The Big Short. It's well-written, easy to read, and character-driven like some of his previous books (Moneyball, The Blind Side). Lewis tells the story of the mortgage  collapse through the (sometimes connected, often not) stories of three people who saw the crash coming early enough to literally bet on it happening. But when it comes to drawing conclusions about the causes of the crisis, it's difficult to accept Lewis's unvarnished (and justified) criticisms of Wall Street. He doesn't address the legal and regulatory framework within which the dangerous financial practices he criticizes made sense. I came away from the book thinking that either everyone involved in the mortgage market must either be stupid, or cruelly shrewd, and the crisis was the collapse of the Ponzi scheme created by the shrewd players (read: big Wall Street investment bankers) to take advantage of the stupid players for their own short term gain (read: fund managers, homeowners who couldn't afford their mortgages, and the institutions that insured the crazy loans those people took out to buy the houses they otherwise couldn't afford, the ratings companies that were tricked into calling those loans \\"safe\\" investments). I came away wondering whether the market is so truly full of dummies, or if their choices actually make sense in light of regulatory manipulations like the Community Reinvestment Act, for example.     Even if he's missing part of the story, though, Lewis is a master at explaining how markets work, portraying entrepreneurial thinking, and showing how incentives lead to (sometimes perverse) market outcomes.

B001NZ1IGG|5.0|As a big fan of \\"grunge\\"-type stuff I was always aware of MLB's existence, their connection to PJ, Temple of the Dog, and Green River, but for some reason didn't buy this album until about a year ago. I liked it when I was first listening to it, but it really grew on me after a while - Andrew Wood described their music as \\"love rock\\", and that's a perfect way of putting it. A lot of their stuff just makes you happy to listen to it. Stylistically, it is quite different from all the aforementioned bands. Despite carrying Gossard, Ament, and Fairweather over from Green River, they sound pretty much nothing like GR...that band was a lot rougher, more Stooges-style...and although Gossard and Ament later went on to form Pearl Jam, and there are some similarities between the music on here and on PJ's \\"Ten\\", overall the music has a very different feel. As some reviewers have stated on here, MLB is kind of like a grungy and non-lame cockrock band...although I feel they sound more like Aerosmith type classic rock than 80s hair metal sort of...it's just fun, happy, cool music, with Wood's flamboyant vocal style setting the tone.    Wood in my opinion sounds quite a bit like Axl Rose, but less screech-y...in addition to the fun rockers on here, there are a couple moving and beautiful tunes that showcase the more tender side of his vocal style. The lyrics are great too. He truly was an extremely talented individual, it really is a shame he doesn't have a larger body of work. It's odd too to think about how Gossard and Ament later went on to do Pearl Jam, who have spent much of their careers striving to move away from commercial aspects of making money, but are here in a band that clearly wanted to be mainstream popular...and if I had to pinpoint the biggest difference between MLB and PJ, it would be that MLB (certain songs excluded) is much less serious, it just cuts loose in a 70s rockin style. It also has less of a punk influence and more of a glam influence. So I'd say this band is less specifically like PJ in style and closer to Aerosmith.    As far as particular songs, Chloe Dancer/Crown of Thorns is indeed a brilliant song - though I much prefer the one with the Chloe Dancer intro to the standalone. Man of Golden Words is a haunting ballad that features the lyrics Temple of the Dog took their name from - it's a beautiful, heartfelt piano piece. Bone China is another favorite, kind of a mid-tempo that finds a balance between the Gentle Groove-type ballads and the Holy Roller-type rockers. The hard-rockin songs are just a brilliant amount of fun, Wood and the rest of the band cut loose in a way that is infectiously pleasing. Capricorn Sister is probably my favorite, but Stardog Champion, Half-Ass Monkey Boy, Holy Roller, and Mindshaker Meltdown are also fantastic...not only that but there really aren't any bad songs on here. In conclusion, a great album, but give it a few spins to get into it - you might not be satisfied if you want more Pearl Jam or Green River, but it's got a great style all its own.

1577314808|5.0|The first time I ever heard about this book was from my dad. He tried to explain to me what the actual meaning behind The Power of Now meant. I thought, after a couple weeks of talking about it, I had grasped the overall purpose and concept, that life was meant to be lived in the moment, the now, and that thinking about the past and future was only progressing you toward a less conscious state. So with that which I had just \\"learned\\" in life, I decided to buy the book and see if I could gather any more helpful knowledge that could keep me grounded in the Now. Once I started reading the book I realized that I had understood the concept correctly like my dad had explained to me, but what I didn't grasp yet was how to utilize it and how much other important information was available.     The beginning of the book explains what our minds are and why we use them in the ways that we do. Ever since I can remember, I have always been that person that argues with myself in my own head and I'm sure that there are many of you that feel this way right now. This section taught me how to shut off the other nagging person in my head. No longer would I have to battle myself anymore in a simple situation of figuring out a yes or no answer. As long as you are identified with your mind, the ego runs your life. Learn to be the witness of your thoughts and emotions.    Tolle believes that the term \\"ego\\" means a false-self, created by unconscious identification with the mind and he couldn't be more spot on. Many of us live this life where we are constantly struggling to be better than others. If we win, we build a sense of pride and accomplishment for ourselves. If we lose, our pride and sense of self is damaged, but our ego still fights to maintain whatever it still can of that pride. Power over others is weakness disguised as strength. You are never satisfied with yourself until you have put someone else in their place, which is below you. This is the false-self that you fuel in orderto feel \\"right\\" in the world. Forgiveness is to offer no resistance to life.    The mind creates an obsession with the future as an escape from the unsatisfactory present. When your mind acts this way, you begin to forget the now and you become lost in time that is not necessary for your development. This doesn't mean that you should not plan for future events or ready yourself, but do not imagine or worry about every possible thing that can or cannot occur in the future. You must do whatever you can NOW to ready yourself. Then you will be able to tackle any situation once it arises. The only important step in life is the one your taking now. Not the steps that you took or are going to take.    There is so much more that this book has to offer that Eckhart Tolle can explain in better words. I tried very hard to find problems with this book, but I couldn't. It is the only book that I have read so far that I can rate a 5. Of course, it is not a book for everyone, but it is a great read and I think that everyone can find something important here as long as they keep open minded as we all should while experiencing life. For those who are interested in spirituality, consciousness and the ego, this is a MUST BUY! And with that, I would like to end my review with a quote from the book:    To complain is always nonacceptance of what is. Leave the situation or accept it.

B00005JMX6|2.0|This film could have been great, but I'm sorry to say it rarely came even close to its potential. First of all, it seemed to be unable to decide if it was a comedy or a coming of age drama. The script tries to present some characters, like Eli (the porn-addicted best friend of the protagonist Matthew) or Kelly (the pornographer villain) as both funny and serious, and so they come across as neither-- Eli is frankly pathetic through most of the film and Kelly seems to operate in an alternate universe where even very public felonies such as abducting a high school student from his classroom in front of two dozen witnesses never elicits a response from the police. Matthew himself spends most of the film just bouncing from scene to scene, and spends remarkably little time actually developing his relationship with Danielle. So there are hijinks that are lost in the general confusion as the plot wanders aimlessly, never really seeming to know where it wants to go.    This is unfortunate, since the basic premise of the film, that a socially awkward young man discovers meaningful love with a woman with a past that his family and society might disapprove of, and along the way is forced to confront his own prejudices about sex and porn, could work as either drama or comedy. But such a story would require a much more carefully written script than this movie has; if you want to see how such a film can be done in a comedic vein, take a look at the old Tom Cruise film [[ASIN:B0018QPPXW Risky Business]].    A second, minor quibble: the unrated version of this film made extensive promises of more skin and more sex, which in a movie about the porn industry really ought to be obligatory (see the brilliant [[ASIN:B00004TQF7 Boogie Nights]] with Burt Reynolds for an excellent treatment of the highs and lows of the business), but in fact delivers neither; the nudity in the unrated version is actually less than many R-rated films I've seen, and I don't recall a single actual sex scene apart from a few brief glimpses from Eli's porn collection. If you're going to make a movie about porn, you need to include some sex. Freed from ratings, they could have here, but chose not to.    I do give the movie two stars for the ending, which does pick up and have a nice, clever twist to it (as well as a good message for young people), but this alone is not enough to save a film whose script I think needed to go through a few more rewrites before being produced. The acting and production values are fine, and Elisha Cuthbert is quite lovely, carrying off the dual role of girl next door and seductive porn queen extremely well. So to repeat: This could have been a great movie; what a pity it wasn't.

Unfortunately, Geddy Lee's sense of melody falters here. There are some great moments, but a lot of the time he sounds like he's forcing the melody to make the lyrics fit the background music. The music is the winner on this album; it deserves 4 1/2 stars. It harkens back to the days of Permanent Waves when the melodies and the music blended perfectly with the mood of the lyrics. Like in Jacob's Ladder or Spirit of Radio. Here the melodies don't take hold, or they take too long to grow on you. They are dense and hard to absorb. Otherwise, this album is a sign of things to come. The next album, CLOCKWORK ANGELS should get it right. VAPOR TRAILS failed in production, TEST FOR ECHO failed in consistency and SNAKES AND ARROWS failed in melodic hooks. And although every one of those albums could've been great, they're all just, well, good. COUNTERPARTS was a winner in production, cohesiveness and it's catchy. It was their best album since MOVING PICTURES. I recommend this one for fans of Rush, but it's not the best way to get to know this group. There's a lot of other albums that are better. If you're new to Rush, my top recommendations (in this order) are:  Moving Pictures  Permanent Waves  Counterparts  2112  Roll The Bones  Signals  Grace Under Pressure  Those are their best. The rest are a toss-up as to which could be called better. They are all good listens but not great albums.

Theories such as Inflation, Relativity and String Theory/M-Theory are only briefly and superficially described. It looks as if the main goal of Hawking was to declare M-theory as the theory to be considered in the next years, and the 160 pages that preceded that declaration were nothing but an incoherent set of excuses for that final declaration.<br /><br />However, I can't say that I learnt nothing from this book, because there was a new thing (at least to me): the \\"Game of Life\\", by John Conway. This is a (computer) model of a two-dimension rule where, with a few simple (fundamental) rules, we can see it generating structure and (apparent) complex laws. This maybe the (almost) perfect analogy on why we should, in the midst of all the complexity of the universe, try to find simple (more fundamental) laws and explanations (even though Hawking does not say it with so many words). 1594202842|5.0|I read Amy Chua's book after work, late into the night because I couldn't put it down and finished it in two sittings, which doesn't happen very often these days.  I loved it and hated it.  At times felt it unashamedly boastful and at other times admired its frankness, humor and insights.  And all the time I couldn't help marveling how someone could make such a complex subject of parenting in America (as an immigrant from a developing country) so simple and interesting and of course, controversial.     At the heart of the \\"Tiger Mother\\" is the tension of raising American children as a Chinese immigrant (though one should keep in mind that Chua herself is a second-generation, Harvard-educated Chinese-American woman married to an equally overachieving American man.)  So Chua's repeated claims of herself as the quintessential \\"Chinese mother,\\" one suspects, are rhetorical ploys to provoke and to entertain, as is her only too obvious willingness to demonize herself in her relationship with her daughters, Sophia and Lulu, and her Occidental husband, Jed.  The point is, she the tiger mother embodies Chinese culture and philosophy of child-rearing, with all its extremes, severity, oppressiveness, unselfishness, devotion, expectations, pains, anxieties, and demand for absolute filial loyalty in return.  What could be more effective, and intriguing, than turning herself, a bright, enlightened Yale Law School professor, into an overgeneralizing, overstating, obsessive, single-minded, tunnel-visioned, unsympathetic character obsessed with her \\"Chinese-ness\\"?    I read the book as a fable with a message for parents in all cultures: When you can, do your best to prepare your children for the future, instill in them a sense of excellence, let them know the feeling of accomplishment, and teach them the importance of hard work.  If Chua had put her message in these words, there wouldn't be this wonderful book that has evoked such strong responses in so many people, would there?I read the book also to realize this:  As a well-to-do second-generation ABC (American Born Chinese), how much more Chua could do for her children than I was able to for my son.  And how elitist her views are about what is considered success and achievement, how privileged a life her daughters are leading and how abundant their opportunities are.  Still, given all the advantages Sophia and Lulu still have to struggle, and struggle mighty hard, to achieve excellence in the instruments they play.  And they need their mother's vision, resolve, resourcefulness and commitment before they are old enough to develop their own.      In the end, Amy Chua holds a mirror up to all parents and challenges us to make the future better by doing our share.  It's a book that doesn't please all but should be read by parents, children, teachers and students alike.  It's a book about tough love.  And Chua's achievement lies in that she has found such a fun way to make everyone think about bringing up our children, one of the most mundane and yet most demanding human enterprises, with the triumphs, pitfalls, ironies, headaches and heartaches and joy that it uniquely brings us.

B00354Y9ZU|5.0|Most people are unhappy, but they do little about it.    If your unhappiness stems from your work, then this is definitely the book to read.  Godin shows us how to become not only an ideal employee, but an indispensible one.    Furthermore, he inspires us to move forward with our ideas and conquer our fears.  Even if you feel you are the type of person who is not the most original and creative, this book will still inspire you to pursue the creativity you do have and make the most of your talents.    Life is sometimes about taking a small step for man-kind, but a giant leap for a man (or woman).  As the author puts it:  \\"The linchpin is an individual who can walk into chaos and create order, someone who can invent, connect, create and make things happen.\\"  Since the dawn of the Industrial age at the beginning of the 19th century, there has been a general agreement between employers and employees: you provide the work, we provide the wages.  Worker rights that expanded in the nearly two centuries since then still exist, but technological advances, compounded by outsourcing have made today's work environment less stable than it has ever been in the modern age.  Even those of us who have a stable job end up doing mindless, boring and unfulfilling tasks with little to no job satisfaction beyond the pay-check.    There is an alternative: to become a linchpin.  A linchpin is a person who cannot be easily replaced, made redundant or outsourced.  This is not due to their position, but their actual abilities.  A linchpin is someone who is enthusiastic when surrounded by naysayers, stands out in a crowd, is the person people turn to when the need help.  This is also the person the company will lay off last, while the rest of the department was let go long ago.  None of this implies the linchpin is always at the top or has a special position, but rather does the `emotional labor' that requires thought and toughness whereas others will choose the easy, obedient path.  Godin states that the linchpin is the artist, using \\"bravery, insight, creativity and boldness to challenge the status quo.\\"  Godin says: \\"We've been culturally brainwashed to believe that accepting the hierarchy and lack of responsibility that comes with a factory job is the one way, the only way and the best way.\\"  Evolution has given us a brain, but since that brain has evolved, this means we carry the baggage of all of our ancestors, including our reptilian ones.  It is this reptilian aspect of our brains which controls our `fight or flight' response and dictates quite a bit of our lives, including our desire for a stable but meaningless job.  It is difficult, but extremely rewarding to overcome this desire, and the first step is recognizing it exists.  Once you establish that you have a fear of failure, standing out and taking risks, you can work to overcome it.    You might also experience anxiety, which is the fear of something before it happens.  Again, this is a product of our evolution and has only been reinforced in the modern age in our working lives.  We have two basic responses to anxiety: assurance or acceptance.  Assurance sounds like the better answer, but it typically is not.  What usually happens is you assure yourself that everything will be alright...as long as things stay the same.  If you accept your anxiety, you will get used to it, and learn to ignore it.  Soon you will learn that nothing will stand in your way.  Becoming a linchpin, as Godin says: \\"You can either fit in or stand out. Not both. You are either defending the status quo or challenging it.\\"  1. Perspective- not only does your outlook shape your reality, but if you take someone else's outlook, or even everyone else's outlook, you can see things as they really are.  2. Acceptance- things typically do not turn out as we expect, and many things change unexpectedly.  Accepting that we have little to no influence over these things is important.  3. Inspiring- if your company is to believe theycannot do without you, you have to be creative, take a fresh approach and every day make a difference.  Inspire yourself!  4. Create- you don't need to cure cancer, make a better wheel or paint the Mona Lisa to be creative.  Linchpins create things that make the world, relationships and everything around them better.  I hope this review has been helpful.

1439102937|4.0|I only read this book because the members of my book club voted for it in a poll. I had no desire to read it, based partly on some assumptions of what the book was about, but based mostly on many bad reviews. Many reviewers complained that the book lacked a plot line. I completely disagree. The story is about three women who, approaching 30 years old, think they are supposed to accomplish something major by that milestone, i.e. marriage and kids. In the course of a year, they come to discover what they really need is to be true to themselves and mindful of their own needs and desires, regardless of their age. Was the plot what one would consider 'high-concept? No, but in my opinion, it didn't matter because it captured and maintained my interest and I was fully invested in the characters. A few reviewers expressed a lack of relatability with the characters. Again, I do not share this opinion. Of the three main characters, I found myself relating to two of them. One had difficulty letting go, jumping in and taking risks, mostly with men, because she was often self-conscious and let fear get in her way. I don't know many people who haven't been paralyzed by fear at some point in their lives. I know I have. The other seemed to have so many blessings, yet she wasn't really happy and felt trapped. It is sometimes difficult to admit to others, and sometimes to ourselves that what is perfect on paper isn't right for us - I have been there as well. (I did not find anything relatable about the third character, a Brazilian drop-dead gorgeous sex goddess who used her sex appeal to get whatever she wanted and whined whenever her friends got attention from men. She was extremely conceited and it bothered me that her ability to be a good friend seemed to be dependent on being able to one-up the other characters. I really didn't see much of a character arc there and her story-line was definitely my least favorite.) Some reviewers complained that the book lacked depth. With all duerespect to those people, the book cover, an image of a stiletto sandal, is highly indicative of the light/fun nature of the story, as is the title. If the reviewer was looking for a deep, thought-provoking novel, he/she should not have chosen that book in the first place. I suppose it could have delved deeper into the psyches of the main characters, but I'm not certain it would have made me enjoy the read any more/less. My only complaint about the book was that a major story-line was concluded through a character telling the reader what happened in a flash-back. I would have much rather have seen it play-out and I definitely felt gypped. I also would have chosen a different happy ending for one of the characters than the one chosen by the author. Despite my premature assumptions, I really enjoyed this book and am happy it was chosen for my book club.

B004CR7TE2|4.0| Fuzzy Wuzzy's Summary:<br /> Recommended with warm fuzzies.<br /><br />\\"LA robe\\" is a series of laptop sleeves originally designed for Apple laptops by the French design company \\"be.ez\\" (pronounced as \\"be easy\\").  Be.ez is obviously proud of their French origins as this laptop sleeve is shipped in a shiny plastic container sleeve with the words \\"French design\\" imprinted on it, and their Web site offers both a French and English language option.  The marketing and branding approach of be.ez's designer sleeves, bags, and cases joins the ranks of Built NY, Timbuk2, and Crumpler for offering subdued, stylish, or funky colors and designs that range from being quietly elegant to loudly trendy.  Even though be.ez originally designed their bags, backpacks, and sleeves just for Apple products, from the Macbook Pro down to the iPad, they have been branching out their product line to include PC laptops, netbooks, and other tablets.<br /><br />This \\"LA robe Club\\" was originally designed for the Macbook Pro 15.4\\".  However, I am using this with my (gasp!!) Dell 15-inch laptop, and it also fits inside this sleeve just fine.  The interior dimensions of this sleeve are: 14.3\\"x9.8\\"x0.95\\".<br /><br />The interior of this sleeve is lined with \\"Low Resilience Polyurethane\\" (LRPu) foam material.  When this sleeve is originally packaged inside its clear plastic container sleeve, dangling on the outside of the package is a sample of the \\"LRPu\\" foam with a label having the big bold words of \\"Low Resilience Polyurethane (LRPu) technology. Feel it!\\", enticing the shopper to feel and press the foam material that this sleeve is lined with.  But \\"Low Resilience Polyurethane\\" is simply the fancy schmancy technical term for memory foam; this is basically a denser firmer version of the memory foam that is now used in memory foam pillows and mattresses.  When you firmly press your fingers into either the interior or exterior of this \\"LA robe\\" sleeve, it feels like pressing into a memory foam pillow or mattress, and the indentations left by your fingers will remain in the LRPu for a long time before the foam returns to its original smooth surface.<br /><br />be.ez's use of LRPu is not unique.  There are other laptop sleeves and bags that make use of LRPu as the main padding; they just usually refer to it by its common name of \\"memory foam\\".  Because of its excellent shock-absorbency, vibration-absorbency, and cushioning characteristics, LRPu is being effectively used for memory foam pillows and mattresses.  But more laptop sleeves use neoprene for its padding.  Neoprene is the material used in wetsuits and thus it confers a fair degree of water-resistance and protection from rain or fluids spilled onto the laptop sleeve.  This \\"LA robe Club\\" sleeve is also very water-repellent as I tried sticking the zipped-up sleeve under the running water of a faucet for awhile.  During a rainstorm, water will soak through the zipper and seams however.<br /><br />The overall construction quality, stitching, seams, external pockets, and interior lining are all high quality.  Jersey fabric covers the exteriors and a smooth soft fleece lining covers the interior of both the sleeve and its two external pockets.  I also have two other laptop sleeves, the [[ASIN:B000NH4EAM Built NY E-LS17-GRN 17-Inch Laptop Sleeve (Green)]] and [[ASIN:B003NVMECU Acme Made Smart Laptop Sleeve PC 15 (Black Chevron)]].  The cool thing about the [[ASIN:B003NVMECU Acme Made Smart Laptop Sleeve PC 15 (Black Chevron)]] is that it has two D-rings on it so that you can attach a shoulder strap to the sleeve to carry it as a shoulder bag.  I like all three of my laptop sleeves, really.  There is such a plethora of laptop sleeves, bags, and cases in similar price ranges that if you are shopping for a sleeve, some of the considerations may boil down to how you like its design and also its appearance.<br /><br />The \\"LA robe Club\\" is described as having a 5mm \\"robe protection thickness\\", fancy schmancy wording for saying that it has a 5mm-thick layer of memory foam.  A 5mm-thick padding is pretty much the norm these days with most manufacturers of laptop sleeves; my other two laptop sleeves also have 5mm of padding.  The padding on this sleeve is typical compared to most laptop sleeves nowadays, regardless of whether the padding is LRPu (memory foam), closed-cell foam, or neoprene.  A laptop sleeve's padding will only protect it from minor bumps and knocks, but there is really not enough padding to cushion against dropping the sleeve onto a floor.<br /><br />Laptop sleeves are really not meant to be carried frequently by themselves through airports or carried loosely in your hand while you walk around.  If you really want good padded protection and room for carrying accessories, get a laptop case instead of a sleeve.  For a similar reason, external pockets and shoulder strap D-rings are very rare on most laptop sleeve designs; the assumption is that you are mainly carrying the sleeve inside another briefcase, bag, backpack, or luggage, and therefore you do not need other storage or carrying options.  The \\"Club\\"-version of this \\"LA Robe Club\\" sleeve differs from its predecessor, the [[ASIN:B001MXD2ZE Be.ez 100775 LA robe Sleeve for New Macbook Pro 15.4 (Black/White)]], in that it has two side-by-side 8\\"x7\\" stretchy neoprene pockets.  These pockets can be used to carry small items like a small AC adapter, USB cords, small wireless laptop mouse, iPod, etc.  I do wish that both of these pockets could be closed up with zippers because items can slip out of the pockets unless you keep the sleeve upright.<br /><br />Overall, this is a great sleeve.  I have yet to find a laptop sleeve that I would give a 5-star rating to because most of them seem quite competitive both in features and price, and unlike larger laptop bags and cases, there is only so much variation of features that you can design into a sleeve. But there are many dozens of colors and print patterns that you can choose from between different manufacturers.  My 'Acme Made' sleeve can convert into a shoulder bag, but it has no storage pockets.  This 'LA robe Club' has storage pockets, but it cannot be used as a shoulder bag (sigh).ncy wording for saying that it has a 5mm-thick layer of memory foam. 0316010669|4.0|Malcolm Gladwell's \\"Blink\\" is an engaging collection of miscellaneous sociocultural and psychological studies, observations, and research that establishes the unconscious brain's focal role in making instinctive, comprehensive and rapid judgments about external stimuli in interactions with others and in everyday to emergency situations. Gladwell brings up an interesting and innovative theory that as human beings, our instincts are one of the most essential and powerful driving forces in our lives, and that our intuition is nevertheless often neglected and manipulated by our outside environment through prejudice, misleading information, first impressions, and superficial appearances. \\"Blink\\" is fascinating as a whole, even though certain articles are a bit too lengthy and meticulous in detail, and appeals to a wide range of people, as almost anybody can relate with the experience of feeling an emotion so strongly that you can't begin to explain or rationally justify. I would definitely recommend this book; Gladwell explains much of the often ignored impulses that human beings naturally experience and furthermore guides the reader to be more confident in their instincts, whether in making business decisions or in getting involved with other people at work or in personal relationships.

0517548232|5.0|ok so we've all heard this left winged argument, \\"if something is bad for society such as cigarettes, high prices, fattening food etc, then why doesn't the government just pass a law against it ?\\"   Hazlitt's book explodes this childish line of thought by separating the goal from the result. An idea or political initiative can have a great stated goal such as \\"lets pass a law against cigarettes when illegal no one or at least far fewer people will smoke, we will all be healthier, health care cost will go down etc\\" this sounds like a great idea but Hazlitt introduces to the equation the problem of unintended consequences. Sure the government can make cigarettes illegal but there are the unintended consequences to this, when ever there is a good people want there will be someone to provide it, much like with illegal drugs today the providers will be violent street gangs, and drug cartels. Street gangs could smuggle tobacco in from other countries or grow it here in America much like what they do now with drugs, the result will be giving them something else they can monopolize the sale of, gives them even more power and money, creates even more violence on the streets, more innocent youths get shot and killed in street gang tobacco selling turf wars etc.  I do not recall seeing the particular example of cigarettes in the book but I just decided to brief on a relevant topic today to illustrate the idea of Hazlitt's book.  Hazlitt goes through many examples of popular goody goody government initiatives that appear all well and do on the outside but have many honorific unintended consequences.  This book will enable you to look at proposed legislation with a completely different set of eyes, seeing through the rhetoric of the stated goal and to the nitty gritty of the likely results   This book along with Irwin Schiff's \\"the biggest con\\" are two of my must haves for anyone pursing self education in free market economics.   5 stars
