0061673730|4.0|Robert M. Pirsig's classic novel is a mix of engaging storytelling and an exploration of how classical Greek philosophy drove the growth of Western civilization; we have mastered the physical world but at the cost of spiritual growth. Those more knowledgeable in philosophy may quibble with his premise, but to me he seems to be one the first modern novelists to advocate taking God out of the temple and recognize that spiritual wholeness was inside us all along.<br /><br />Story: The book intertwines three stories; the first follows a college professor struggling to become whole again after a devastating psychotic breakdown. His story provides structure and context for a lengthy introduction to the growth of Western philosophy and what we have gained and sacrificed as a result. The third story details his attempts to reconnect with his son after his breakdown shattered his family. The blend of engaging story, philosophical introduction, and spiritual journey is why the book has endured as a classic philosophical/new age novel for the last quarter-century (this is my overview).<br /><br />Spiritual/metaphysical content: Medium. Pirsig's theme explores Quality and Truth with a capital T.  Interestingly, he equates Quality to God only twice in the book (Chapters 21 and xx), and he implies that the reconciliation of Quality and Truth results in Zen but never explicitly states that point. However, in my opinion that's the driving force behind the work--the need for Western civilization to get back to a state of spiritual wholeness, enlightenment, God, Zen, dharma--the words don't matter. Perhaps it was necessary in the early 1970s to use the terms of classical philosophy in order to be taken seriously, but the core message of the book is clear: We a lost a part of our soul by embracing Western philosophy.  Western civilization has gained mastery over the world, but \\"lost the ability to be a part of the world and not an enemy of it.\\"<br /><br />Although Pirsig sidesteps the effect of religion on Western development, I feel this fracturing of our world view was exacerbated by the spread of Christianity. People were encouraged to stop looking inside of themselves for truth and let the Church direct their spiritual growth and define their values and ethics. I'm not certain, but Pirsig may have been one of the first 20th-century American novelists to popularize the notionthat Western thought took a wrong turn more than 2,300 years ago and that exploring Eastern philosophy may help fill a spiritual void for many people.<br /><br />My take: I'll admit, the rhetorical sections were a hard slog at times. I felt like I was reading a doctoral candidate's dissertation without the footnotes. However, I learned a great deal about Western philosophy and how we got to where we are as a society.  Greek philosophers, primarily Aristotle, moved away from the idea of wholeness and individual excellence - Quality - toward dividing the way we see the world into mind and matter, which enabled Western cultures to master the realm of matter. However, spiritual growth was sacrificed to technological innovation; we a lost a part of our soul by embracing Western philosophy. Despite the dense philosophical sections, I loved the book 20 years ago, and after rereading it I still love it today. The blend of engaging story, great writing, philosophical exploration, and spiritual journey is why the book has endured as a classic new age novel for the last quarter-century.estern development, I feel this fracturing of our world view was exacerbated by the spread of Christianity. People were encouraged to stop looking inside of themselves for truth and let the Church direct their spiritual growth and define their values and ethics. I'm not certain, but Pirsig may have been one of the first 20th-century American novelists to popularize the notionthat Western thought took a wrong turn more than 2,300 years ago and that exploring Eastern philosophy may help fill a spiritual void for many people.    My take: I'll admit, the rhetorical sections were a hard slog at times. I felt like I was reading a doctoral candidate's dissertation without the footnotes. However, I learned a great deal about Western philosophy and how we got to where we are as a society.  Greek philosophers, primarily Aristotle, moved away from the idea of wholeness and individual excellence - Quality - toward dividing the way we see the world into mind and matter, which enabled Western cultures to master the realm of matter. However, spiritual growth was sacrificed to technological innovation; we a lost a part of our soul by embracing Western philosophy. Despite the dense philosophical sections, I loved the book 20 years ago, and after rereading it I still love it today. The blend of engaging story, great writing, philosophical exploration, and spiritual journey is why the book has endured as a classic new age novel for the last quarter-century.

I'm usually bawling my eyes out because someone died and ending is devastatingly tragic - not because it's happy! I was in awe of the beautiful buildings in Paris, and my stomach was in knots every time St. Clair tried to climb stairs. There was not one flaw with this book that I can find. Others surely can, but Stephanie Perkins has definitely mad me a raving mad fangirl. Because that's all I could think about every time he or one of his books were mentioned!      Please, please, please, Hollywood, make a wonderful movie out of this one day so I can swoon visually as well as mentally?       I could probably gush for hours on end about this book, and I really can't even come up with a coherent review that I'm happy with. Regardless, if you haven't read this yet, do it now. Immediately. It's a refreshing, toe-curling, goosebump-inducing romance that takes place in one of the most romantic cities in the world. Paris! Boarding schools! Cute boys! What isn't to love?

031603438X|4.0|NOTE - This review contains references to incidents which are familiar to many Rolling Stones fans, but which may be considered \\"spoilers\\" for newer fans. Please proceed at your own risk.<br /><br />This is a very hard review to write for me. I have been a huge fan of the Rolling Stones and Keith Richards in particular my entire life. The book is very interesting - Keith's early life, the formation of the Stones, picking up the 5 string technique, substance-fueled exploits around the world, the legendary recording sessions at Muscle Shoals Alabama and in the basement of the Chateau for Exile - it's all here. I've been looking forward for some time for Keith to \\"lift the veil\\" on the Stones and his life, and he does here. If you are a fan of the Stones, rock, blues, or any music for that matter, this book is a must read. The temperament of a gifted artist is outlined here in great detail - the good, the bad, and the ugly.<br /><br />In recent years Keith has been cultivating a \\"benevolent Rock and Roll grandfather survivor\\" image, which is all very well and good, but about halfway through the book things begin to get disturbing. The children come along. Keith and Anita Pallenberg, both junkies by this time, have 3 children. Keith leaves for long stints on the road, as his job requires, knowingly leaving the children with their drug-addicted mother again and again. One child dies, and the surviving son, Marlon, spends long periods out of school. Keith takes him on the road with the Stones at age 5 to \\"take care of him,\\" but Marlon instead ends up taking care of his heroin-addicted father in a succession of anonymous hotel rooms throughout the tour. Marlon essentially runs wild, ostensibly watched by the rest of the Stones entourage. Marlon even learns to clean up his father's drugs when the \\"men in blue\\" show up. The younger daughter, Angela, is left at home with Pallenberg through her drug-fueled rages, police busts, etc. I lost count of how many fires were started due to \\"faulty wiring.\\" The drugs keep flowing, the kids are essentially ignored for long periods, and the Stones keep pumping out classic albums which seem, in Keith's view, to make everything all right. It does not.<br /><br />Keith finally \\"cleans up\\" from heroin after several tries (although booze and other drugs are always around) and leaves Pallenberg, but the children are still left with her for long periods during her continued drug binges.  Meanwhile Keith fights with Mick about Jagger's solo career, Keith starts his own band the X-Pensive Winos (which by and large comes across as petty one-upmanship), and the Stones continue to be the biggest band in Rock and Roll. Meanwhile Keith's family continues to suffer.<br /><br />All of this is hard to swallow, but swallow it we do in the name of \\"artistic temperament.\\"  The petty selfishness keeps coming after the 1970's, however.  Even though the Stones' large entourage often has its families in tow, recording sessions don't start until 9pm, 10pm, and then 1am because that's when Keith is ready. They record all night and sleep all day because that is how Keith wants it. The immature selfish Rock Star act goes on and on, but everyone puts up with it because \\"Keith is Keith\\" and because the Stones keep piling up the classics and adding to their legend. A wink and a grin only goes so far for the reader, however.<br /><br />As the story continues we begin to ask ourselves, \\"When is enough enough?\\" Is it when Keith trashes Bill Wyman for wanting to retire after working so closely with him for 30 years? Is it when Keith meets future wife Patti's family for the first time and he shows up wasted and makes a huge, uncomfortable scene, smashing a guitar on the table in front of everyone?  Is it when Keith keeps devoting pages and pages to his exploits with the X-Pensive winos and getting back at Mick after giving such short thrift to the death of his own child? Is it during the horrific scene when the young Marlon sneaks upstairs to see Pallenberg's boyfriend shot dead in the head after playing Russian Roulette?<br /><br />After all of this detailed debauchery, the end of the book feels as if it was hurriedly pieced together, and it essentially falls apart. Sordid tales are one thing, but sloppy writing is another.<br /><br />The story of the artistic temperament is well documented here as well as in the stories of other gifted geniuses. Soaring art emerges for the world while personal relationships and families suffer. But we all put up with it \\"for the sake of art.\\" Maybe the trick is not to look too closely behind the veil, because although we may love the art and the legend, we might not ending up liking the real person beneath very much.ot dead in the head after playing Russian Roulette? Lisbeth Salander is a classic character and deserves every page she gets.<br /><br />Some notes on reading this book:<br /><br />1. The Swedish justice system is a bit different from the justice system in the U.S., as will become apparent during the trial sequence. The variations are very interesting and add quite a bit of flavor to the proceedings. Things are done differently in Sweden, so it's best to accept it and learn. The miscarriage of justice, both in the Courts and in the media, is a major theme in all three books.<br /><br />2. Since some of the Swedish names used sound very similar, I made a few notes while reading to keep them all straight. The characters distinguish themselves as things go along, so don't dwell too much on it. You should be able to identify the characters by their mannerisms and by what they do.<br /><br />3. Pay attention and keep moving. Some writers \\"dumb down\\" their stories in the interest of mass appeal, and keep going over the same territory again and again to drum it into your head. Larsson does not. He challenges you to keep up and rewards you richly for doing so.<br /><br />The Girl Who Kicked the Hornet's Nest is a terrific and satisfying end to the outstanding Millennium Trilogy. The only problem is that it has to end here due to Stieg Larsson's untimely death. Larsson proves himself here to be a true master, and the entire series is destined to be a classic.<br /><br />Hornet's nest rating: A. Millennium Trilogy rating: A+.: A. Millennium Trilogy rating: A+.

B000002OZY|4.0|I gave this package 4 stars only because you really should get the two-disc set recently released with demos and alternate takes on it.  That's a much greater value.    If Pete Townshend really does think The Who will be measured primarily by \\"Tommy,\\" he really doesn't have a good grasp of his own oeuvre.  By most people's count, Who's Next is The Who's masterpiece.     \\"Tommy\\" is the first, and maybe the greatest of the rock operas.  Released the same year as The Kinks' \\"Arthur\\" and The Moody Blues' \\"On the Threshold of a Dream,\\" it is part of The Who's best creative period, which ended with \\"Quadrophenia.\\"      It is also very much a transition album for The Who.  In musical style, it is the last album to feature their wonderful three-part Beach Boys-like harmonies and '60's pop style.  While touring \\"Toommy\\" all over the world, Roger Daltrey (maybe the best male singer in pop) developed a more full-thoated style of singing, which he featured to good effect on all subsequent Who albums.  The synthesizers which made their first appearance on Who's Next also, of course, absent here.    Townshend has in many interviews also credited bass player extraodinaire John Entwistle with playing a pivotal role in putting \\"Tommy\\" together.  He said he had the various ideas in his head, but they didn't gel until Entwistle popped up with \\"Cousin Kevin.\\"  The expanded edition has on it Entwistle's first treatment for \\"Cousin Kevin\\" -- \\"Cousin Kevin Model Child.\\"  Both songs put in the two sides of kevin's personality -- the model child in front of the adults and the beast within.  Entwistle's \\"Uncle Ernie\\" is just pure bass pounding and was one of The Who's big numbers live.    Unlike Townshend's later efforts which are self-contained, \\"Tommy\\" builds on earlier songs, especially tracks from 1967's \\"The Who Sell Out,\\" their parody of British BBC radio.  \\"See Me, Feel Me\\" is built on a similar phrase in the earlier album's \\"I Can't Reach You.\\"  \\"It's a Boy\\" comes right out of a song\\"Glow Girl\\" which was recorded earlier and appears on The Who's 1973 collection \\"Odds and Sods\\" (which also contains a slightly different version of \\"Cousin Kevin Model Child\\").  The main theme of \\"Sparks\\" and \\"Underture\\" (admittedly filler) as well as the emphasis on Christmas (a couple of early treatments of \\"Chistmas\\" are on the two-CD set) came right out of the two-song cycle \\"Rael\\" (one song of which appeared on \\"Sell Out\\").  Thus, you can see the development of Townshend's ideas over time.    On the negative side, \\"Tommy\\" is, thematically, somewhat dated.  It is built on the mystic teachings of Meher Baba, which is credited as \\"Avatar\\" on the album.  As other reviewers have noted, however, it still stands up well as a total work with classics like \\"I'm Free,\\" \\"Pinball Wizard,\\" \\"Uncle Ernie,\\" and of course \\"See Me, Feel Me.\\"    If you are putting together a collection of great albums, this one has to be in it.

0786888628|4.0|The Coldest War is a bit odd for a war history. It's a nearly equal mix of larger geopolitical struggles driving the war and stories of individual soldiers along the lines of Stephen Ambrose's works. It's not a bad mix, and at times it's nice to get both perspectives on the war. Still, at times I felt like the going back and forth left part of the story untold. For example, the book spends surprisingly little time discussing General Ridgeway's life and background. It's not even clear what happened to him after the war. Then, we're introduced to infantry troops who, on short notice, become major protagonists. Hopefully, this means the book will appeal even to readers who already have some familiarity with the Korean War. Overall, the book is well written and accessible, so even readers unfamiliar with the history can enjoy it.

0312370849|4.0|Sarah's Key is a novel about a relatively unknown piece of history - the French involvement and responsibility for the killing of 13,000 Jewish families.  Taking orders from the Gestapo, the French acted on their own, with their own police gathering the Jews and leading them to their deaths in Auschwitz.<br /><br />The story is told from two vantage points.  One takes place in 1942 and is told by the Jewish child Sarah who is rounded up from her home with her parents by the police.  She hides her brother in a cabinet thinking he will be safe there, not realizing she will be unable to get back to him to set him free.  The reader shares Sarah's horrific trials as she is put in a French internment camp, escapes, and runs for her life.<br /><br />Alternating chapters are from the  viewpoint of an American woman in France named Julia in the present.  She is fascinated with Sarah's history once she learns that her husband's family moved into Sarah's family's apartment once they vacated it.  She is obsessed with finding Sarah and learning what happened to her.  She is also in the midst of marital crisis.  We get to know her daughter Zoe, too precocious for her years.  She seems like 11 going on 40.  We also get to know her narcissistic husband, Bertrand, who has been having an affair with another woman for most of their marriage.  The search for Sarah takes Julia across the world and she learns the history of France's role in the extermination of the Jews.  We also learn the horrifying secret that Bertrand's family has been hiding for 60 years.<br /><br />Somehow, despite the horror, poignancy, and occasional lightness of the story, Julia and Zoe's dialogues don't always ring true.  I have never known a scorned woman to act so unresponsively to her husband's infidelity as Julia does.  It does not ring true. She also does not seem to have a clue when he is being unfaithful though she describes the thick and heavy perfume of Bertrand's lover which is on his clothes and person.<br /><br />I was riveted by the story of Sarah and the history of the Vel d'hiv round-up. Overall, this is a stunning work, marking the beginning of what I'm sure will be the most epic series of the next decade. Just ask him.<br /><br />Kawasaki's book, \\"The Art of the Start,\\" literally became the bible of startup entrepreneurs looking for buy-in along Silicon Valley's Sand Hill Road VC set, while catapulting Kawasaki to essential speaker status at many of the Bay Area's high-tech conferences of the last half decade.<br /><br />In this, his tenth book, the author, speaker and former evangelist for Apple computer, takes us on a journey to \\"Enchantment\\"; that special quality that takes companies from necessary to can't live without status. Steve Jobs' company is, of course, at the top of the enchantment list, but others like internet shoe emporium Zappos and even Queen Latifah qualify, too.<br /><br />Along the way, Kawasaki points out rules, guideposts, axioms and techniques all designed to help your company increase its engagement, reputation and success in being the top-of-mind, go-to company in its unique product or marketing space. \\"Enchantment\\" quickly covers a wide array of methods to help insure success; from how to dress, to Tweeting parameters (tweet often, repeat your tweets, did I tell you I run Alltop?) to learning how to present (shorten, rehearse, repeat). Most of Kawasaki's techniques make perfect sense and he sometimes takes both sides of the argument to show just how rules have exceptions to themselves as well.<br /><br />The problem may just be Kawasaki himself. At times, it seems like the author is just trying too hard to cement his reputation as a \\"high-tech guru.\\" He continues to use his trademarked phrases from previous efforts (\\"bull shiitake,\\" \\"10-20-30') as gives us \\"Guy's rules\\" for nearly everything. Unlike contemporaries such as Seth Godin or David Meerman Scott, Kawasaki seems unable to proffer advice without a heavy dose of self-promotion (Did I tell you I met Richard Branson?)<br /><br />If there is one lesson that the author dispenses that would also serve him well, it might just be humility. If you can get past that, this is otherwise a good book.wise a good book.

B00005R24G|5.0|To see two great actors face off in a film like this is a joy to behold. 'Sleuth' provides this golden opportunity, especially when both actors are at the top of their game. You would think that Lawrence Olivier's grand theatrical acting style and Michael Caine's more straightforward acting style would clash, but in 'Sleuth' they blend wonderfully. It helps that Anthony Shaffer's outstanding screenplay (based on his popular stage play) gives these two actors a delicious cat and mouse game to engage in.     Olivier is a famous mystery novelist who invites Caine, his wife's hairdresser, to his estate to offer him an intriguing proposal. You see, Caine is sleeping with Olivier's wife and Olivier wants Caine to, as he says, \\"take her off my hands\\". To reveal anymore would spoil all the fun. Just enjoy the twists and turns of this delightful story.     Translating a stage play like this to the big screen can be a tricky proposition, but director Joseph L. Mankiewicz pulls this off splendidly. Ken Adam (who worked on many of the James Bond films) does a brilliant job designing the sets, especially the incredible variety of games that are featured which vividly illustrates the obsession with games playing that Olivier's character has. I don't know if Ken Adam found them in shops or invented them himself, but they certainly are ingenious. You try solving a 500 piece jigsaw puzzle that's completely blank!    If you purchase this DVD, DO NOT get the 1997 Anchor Bay edition. It has poor picture quality, it was not formatted properly (1.66:1, you can tell by looking at the opening credits), you have to flip the disc halfway through the film to watch the second part of the film and the audio is awful. I had to turn the volume all the way up just to understand the dialogue! You should get the 2002 Anchor Bay edition. The picture quality is improved, it's formatted properly (1.85:1), the audio is clear and solid and it also has an interview with Anthony Shaffer that was recordedshortly before he died. Oh, yeah...the film and bonus material are all on one side of the disc! I've owned both editions and, trust me, the 2002 edition is much, much better. I hope you enjoy this wonderful film as much as I always do.

It's not hard to see where this would go.  (some spoilers) She goes from being an oh-so-sweet mommy's nice girl to doing drugs and engaging in deviant sexual behavior. They use Winona Rider as a starlet gone wrong (read as flipped her lid) to illustrate the path she's heading down. You can get some really good photos with this lens, just don't plan on having the luxury of zooming and cropping because the quality is not quite that high. 0307408833|3.0|Update:    After looking at the author's website and her recipes posted in MarthaStewart.com, I've removed one star from my rating. The online recipes are different from the ones on the book. For the vanilla frosting recipe, for example, everything was the same except for the amount of the soy milk, which was half of what was indicated in the book. I think is indicates that the recipe in the book was erroneous. However, the author does not admit this. It's not even mentioned in the FAQ I linked to below. I feel bad the author treats her readers this way. We deserve the correct, tested recipes. If mistakes were made, admit them and move on. No wonder so many people failed in their attempts to make the frosting :-(      **************    I saw this cookbook at the bookstore and immediately fell in love with the photographs, and the promise of delicious baked goods made without gluten and/or wheat, dairy, eggs, and sugar.     This was important to me, because we recently discovered my son was allergic to wheat, gluten, dairy, eggs and peanuts. I didn't want him to miss out on his favorite treats in spite of his food allergies, so I set out to find the best GFCFEF recipes for baking.    When I got home and had the chance to see the reviews for this book, I got nervous. It seems people were trying these recipes and failing.    I'm not at all surprised that the recipes aren't exactly what they use in the bakery. I mean, come on, people. Baking for a few people is far different than baking for hundreds. Bakeries tend to measure ingredients by weight, for example. However this isn't realistic for most home bakers.    I do expect, however, the recipes to have been tested several times before publication.     I tried my first recipe in this book with a little trepidation and a lot of hope. My first attempt were the brownies. I didn't have gar-fava, so I used rice flour instead. I also substituted canola oil for the coconut oil. The results I got were amazing. They were so delicious and moist, I had to shoo my non-allergic daughters away so their brother would have some left over.    My next attempt, the banana bread, wasn't as successful, however. Like others who've tried the teacake recipes, mine wouldn't cook in the middle. The loaf rose beautifully, but then I took it out of the oven, thinking it was done (the toothpick looked clean though moist) and it promptly sank in the middle. When I sliced a knife in the middle, it turned out to be very wet and raw. So I placed it back in the oven for another 5, 10, 15 minutes. It's still in the oven now, as I type this.    That said, the banana chocolate chip mini-muffins I made with my leftover batter turned out perfectly.    I'm not entirely blaming the recipe. I've had the exact same thing happen when I baked a regular banana bread recipe years ago. I actually blame the bananas. Their moisture content is probably too unpredictable or something. So maybe next time I'm going to use the same recipe but bake muffins instead. Or increase the amount of flour by 1/3 cup or so.    I love that the recipes are not only allergen-free but are healthy as well. I like the idea of using plenty of coconut oil, because I do believe coconut is good for our health. Maybe that's why the author is so slim despite (it appears) eating a lot of sweets. I'm also attracted by the prospect of having my sweet treats without using refined sugar.    As with other allergen-free cookbooks, you'll need specialty flours to bake the recipes. That's just the way it is when you want to bake but are constrained to use wheat flour, eggs, butter and cow's milk.    By the way, if you can't find the ingredients, the author has some suggested substitutions and other tips on her FAQ page at []    I'm looking forward to trying more recipes from this cookbook. I can't wait until I get my hands on some unscented coconut oil to try out the famous Babycakes cupcake and frosting. Stay tuned!

B0041KKYBA|5.0|The financial collapse that occurred throughout the world in the fall of 2008 was an event some three decades in the making.  The deregulating of financial institutions in America, which allowed for rampant speculation, ridiculous home mortgages that people couldn't possibly pay back in a hundred years, and all the various financial ponzi schemes, eventually turned out to be a total disaster.  And this entire debacle, the worst thing to happen to the world, wars and all, since the Great Depression, is what is looked into in INSIDE JOB, an extremely powerful documentary from director Charles Ferguson, whose 2007 debut documentary NO END IN SIGHT, squarely delineated our faults in Iraq.    Treading a lot of the same ground as Michael Moore's 2009 broadside CAPITALISM: A LOVE STORY, but without the populist rhetoric that went along with it, INSIDE JOB takes a long, hard look at the hundreds of individuals and dozens of financial institutions around the world who, thanks to deregulation, caused the financial collapse that eventually led to the national debt of the United States to double, for thirty million people all over the world to lose their jobs, and all of this at a cost estimated to be at least $20 trillion, if not more.  Between the end of the Great Depression and the beginning of the Reagan era, the American economy, while it did rack up enormous debt, was still humming along--the envy of the world as it were.  Once Ronald Reagan and his Wall Street honchos took over the American economy, however, the confluence of money and political influence drove to the near-total elimination of all financial regulations and the brainwashing of the American people that limitless wealth was there for the taking.  From the start, there were signs that America would have to pay at some point.  Wall Street, thanks to risky stock investors and speculators, became the world's biggest casino, with real estate and Internet stocks leading the way, and eventually leading America tothe precipice of a financial Armageddon.  Any attempt on the part of any lawmaker in Congress to revive regulatory rules was stopped by these financial heavyweights, whose influence extended all the way to the White House via the Federal Reserve.    The plummeting stock market collapse of September 15, 2008 should have provided the chance to reform the system.  But as documented by Ferguson, through interviews with former New York state attorney general and governor Elliot Spitzer, former Federal Reserve chief Paul Volcker, author Charles Morris, French finance minister Christine LaGarde, and others, little to nothing was being done to ensure that this would never happen again.  And while Reagan, Bush Sr., and Bush the Younger get a lot of the blame, Bill Clinton and Barack Obama don't exactly get out of this unscathed.  Many of the same people who caused the crisis are still in place; and unless they're removed, or put on trial and sent to jail, nothing will get done to prevent another financial meltdown in the future.    While Ferguson, an academic from MIT, is by no means a product of either the working class or the Far Left like Moore is, he nevertheless delivers as searing an indictment as Moore did in CAPITALISM: A LOVE STORY, though he doesn't advocate the tearing down of the entire capitalist structure like Moore.  He lays out all the facts in a very levelheaded, fair, and balanced examination that is, however, quite dismaying at the very least, and downright infuriating at its worst.  The loss of protections and rules have cost so many millions of Americans their jobs, their homes, and their life savings; and yet there are those who insist, even unto this very day, that financial deregulation worked.  Evidence clearly shows that just the opposite was the case.  And it wasn't just America that suffered from its own deregulation morass; the entire world has suffered, and continues to keep suffering.    The same kind of independent thinking that drove NO END IN SIGHT clearly drives INSIDE JOB, and a more low-key but still populist rage is there too.  We need to remove banks and predatory lenders from the political process and put them on a long but tight leash.  More importantly, we need to permanently debunk the myth of financial deregulation being beneficial to the world, a myth still being perpetrated by our big financial institutions and those in the GOP, the Tea Party, and the Far Right media, if the world is to avoid going over the edge into a financial black hole from which, the next time, there may be no way out of whatsoever.

0439023521|3.0|There are plenty of eloquently written synopses of this novel here on Amazon, so I will spare you another re-telling.  However, I would like to share why the title of my review has a double-meaning:    I am indeed hungry to continue reading the trilogy--I read this book in one evening and will most likely knock the other two out this weekend.  The premise of the book was great, and with respect to the concepts of government abuse and the uglier aspects of human nature, I could easily draw parallels to our world today.  I found the author's writing style to be engaging and relatable, much like her characters in the story.  And due to the abrupt, cliffhanger-ish ending I want to know \\"the rest of the story.\\"    This reminds me of why I'm still hungry...    Maybe I've watched too many Tarantino movies or read too many Stephen King novels, but I was not blown away by the \\"goriness\\" or \\"brutality\\" that so many reviewers mentioned.  It's the overall theme of humanity gone wrong that I find most scary, not the physical acts of atrocity that were committed in the book.  That's innate fight-or-flight right there.  The heroine's struggle to survive didn't leave an indelible mark on my soul either, nor did the potential \\"love triangle\\" constructed by the author.  I appreciate a great romance as much as the next reader, but I distinctly recall thinking that I would've enjoyed the whole \\"first kiss vs. real first kiss with feelings\\" if I were still a teenaged girl.    For me the mark of an engrossing book is if I lose all track of time and place, and the world falls away to the point where I don't hear or see anything but the characters.  I know many readers gave this book 5 stars, which for me means this book would've produced some sort of paradigm shift.  For me it was an enjoyable read, but I could also read it while performing other tasks, so instead I gave it 3 stars.
